how modest jim won

the  prize

by william lawrence

author of the awakening

es-i remember why they call the big boss

  jl modest jim  said the old timer it
started way back twenty years ago

  i was in my third apprentice year when jim
hadley came to work here--a quiet bashful boy
his father had died and he was forced to leave
grammar school and go to work

  he was never among the groups of boys dodg-
ing the boss or watching the clock nor mixed up in
anything that wasnt strictly business and he
never fooled away -his time with the bunch after
hours so we left him pretty much to himself we
called him modest jim

  one day old man adams who owned this out-
fit in those days came out into the shop and tacked
up a sign over the foremans desk

  it seemed that the old man and his designer
had run into a stone wall or what was greek to
most of us kids those days and was offering a prize
of fifty dollars to anyone who solved the problem
he must have been up against it or he never would
have asked ouc bunch for help

  about two weeks later after the rest of us
had forgotten all about it the old man rushed
out to jim and fairly pushed five crisp ten dollar
bills into his hand he had solved the problem

  when the foreman asked him how he did it
he replied oh it wasnt anything great only
a fresh brain on a stale subject thats all he
ever said about it but do you know what that
boy had been doing hed been studying with the
international correspondence schools in his spare
time no wonder he got ahead

  and he went right up and up and up until
today he is the big boss and thp rest of us are
just rbout where we started hes still modest
jim but hes earning five times as much as i am

  take my advice lad and follow along in jims
footsteps dont wait until its too late to get the
special training that is so essential to success

  employers everywhere are looking for men like
modest jim-men who want to get ahead-who
are willing to devote a par of their spare time to
training for advancement

  how much longer are you going to wait before
taking the step that is bound to bring you a better
job and more money

   isnt it better to make your start now than to
 wait a year or five years and then wish you had
 it certainly is

   no matter where you live the i c s will come
 to you no matter what your handicaps or how
 small your means we have a plan to meet your
 circumstances no matter how limited your previous
 education the simply-written wonderfully-illus-
 trated i c s lessons make it easy to learn no
 matter what career you may choose some one of
 the  i c s courses will surely suit your needs

   this is all we ask without cost without obli-
 gating yourself in any way put it up to us to prove
 how we can help you just mark and mail this

an easy way to
   remove dandruff

  if you want plenty of thick beautiful glossy
silky hair do by all means get rid of dandruff for
it will starve your hair and ruin it if you dont

   the best way to get rid of dandruff is to dissolve
it to do this just apply a little liquid arvon at
night before retiring use enough to moisten the
scalp and rub it in gently with the finger tips

  by morning most if not all of your dandruff
will be gone and three or four more applica-
tions should completely remove every sign and
trace of it

   you will find too that all itching of the scalp
will stop and your hair will look and feel a hun-
dredtimes better you can get liquid arvon at
any drug store a four-ounce bottle is usually
all that is needed

it is fortunate for hupmobile owners
that our engineers have large manu-
facturing facilities at their exclusive
command

in the hupmobiles own plants motor
transmission clutch axles and other
essential parts are built to hupmobile
design for the exclusive use of the
hupmobile

no compromise of good designing or
fine manufacturing is ever forced upon
our engineers they are entirely free
from limitations they might meet if they
were dependent for some vital parts
on outside manufacturing sources

through years of experience they have
developed a perfect harmony among
working parts that tends to increase the

economy and efficiency of individual
units and of the entire assembly

this may explain hupmobile perform-
ance- the kind of performance that
means sailing up the average low-gear
hill on high gear and plugging
through sand and mud on high

the kind of pick-up that gets you
away and free from city-street snarls
brilliant performance not only when
the car is new but as long as it lasts
with the very minimum of tinkering
and adjusting

this may explain also economy rec-
ords that stand almost alone among
cars of higher and lower price alike
and such a length of life that it seems
a hupmobile has no wear-out point

    cord tires on all models prices f  b detroit-revenue tax extra

author of the king in check a secret society etc

chapter i

we can reconstruct the whole of human history

ell you know how the firm of
grim ramsden  ross had its
beginnings we have had to
use all our wits to save ourselves
from being used by one government against
another rivals for political power have
tried to employ us for their own ends and
have succeeded more than once youd
need the brains of an archangel and satan
combined to see through all the proposals
that get brought to us but theres reason-
able money in it and its good fun weve
cracked a hard-boiled egg or two and spilled
some beans

strange gave grim sole charge of the
near-east end purchased narayan singhs

moses and mrs aintree copyright  by talbot mundy

discharge from the army and left the two
of them in cairo planting jeremy in
london

  strange and i went to the states where
with the aid of his old office staff we ham-
mered through the organizing and incor-
poration and while we were in the middle
of all that the first view forward came
over the wires from grim strange was in
washington

  meet john brice new york s s olympic ok
follow up grim

  i boarded the olympic along with the
pilot away outside sandy hook it was
early morning but brice was pacing the
deck with a companion and i observed
them both for several minutes after a stew-
ard had told me who they were

  brice was short with a close-clipped gray
beard wiry dried out and resolute-looking

copyright  by the ridgway company in the united states and great britain all rights reserved 


adventure

he stepped forward gamely with a decided
limp and made way for other people ap-
parently unconsciously i guessed his age
between fifty and fifty-five

  his companion was about two inches
taller angular and lean a scot whichever
way you viewed him a fellow with one of
those noses that warn you not to waste
persuasion on him hell decide for himself
he had heavy sandy-colored eyebrows
broad shoulders and a sort of side-swing
that went with them he wore a heather-
colored highland tam-o-shanter and a small
leather bag slung over his shoulder by a
strap the steward told me his name was
allison and that he had shared brices
stateroom on the voyage

  i introduced myself and brice chose one
of those leather-upholstered corners in the
smoking-room and curled himself up cross-
legged staring at me frankly and fairly
he made no effort to disguise the fact that
he was making an inventory of my pros
and cons his companion seated next to
him did the same perhaps not quite so
sympathetically

  we met grim in cairo brice began at
last allison and i are egyptologists
weve been nineteen years in egypt on be-
half of the british museum the egyp-
tian law as regards antiquities is strict and
no kind of excavating is allowed without a
permit which is only granted to represen-
tatives of such institutions as the british
museum the metropolitan museum of
new york and so on all discoveries
have to be reported and nothing may be
removed from the spot without permission
in writing

  allison and i have specialized in the
period during which the israelites were in
egypt with particular referenceto moses
very little is known about moses histori-
cally speaking in the biblical account not
even the name is given of the egyptian
princess who is said to have discovered him
among the reeds it was our purpose if
possible to throw light on all that period
establishing as many facts as might be-

  and exploding a number of absur-r-d
traditions put in allison weve dis-
proved more than yed imagine possible

  we have established the fact definitely
that moses did exist brice continued
whether or not one man wrote the deca-
logue whether or not one individual led
that horde of israelites and established the

ten commandments and the law we have
proved that moses did exist he lived
at a time that corresponds roughly with that
of the israelitish colony in egypt our
proofs however are missing

  do you expect to find them in new
york i asked youll find moses family
is not extinct

  our quest has nothing to do with
jews brice answered the most as-
tonishing thing about the jewish race is
their indifference to the monuments of their
own past allison and i discovered a
temple in which moses undoubtedly studied
that learning of the egyptians with which
he is credited we found the symbols and in-
signia that he used in the performance of
the secret rites those have been stolen
and it might occur to you that jews would
be the logical people on whom suspicion
should rest but jews have nothing what-
ever to do with it

  we kept our discovery secret it was
so important and the finds themselves
were of such intrinsic value that it seemed
wisest to close up everything until we could
confer with the proper authorities regard-
ing the disposal of what could be easily
removed

     one of our staff was an abyssinian
fc an individual named gulad who had
been educated in america he had
served us faithfully for nine or ten years
and you know the proverb tf a comrade
in arms is what you need buy a nubian
slave if you want to grow rich buy an
abyssinian that fellow gulad was the
finest steward of resources that we ever had
nothing escaped his notice nobody-
not even a desert bedouin-could steal
from him and he was sufficiently educated
to appreciate the importance of antiquities
aside from the mere price they might bring
in the open market

  we had entered the temple through a
hole in one corner of the wall after tunneling
through sand for more than a hundred
yards shoring up the tunnel with timber
as we went most of the important finds
were in a great stone chest weighing several
tons almost the exact counterpart of the
so-called sarcophagus in the kings cham-
ber in the great pyramid only having a lid
that fits tightly in place the chest in
the great pyramid has no lid

  whatever had been found outside the
moses and mrs aintree



stone chest we placed inside it except for
one small item then with the aid of
gulad and ten men we lifted the lid back
cemented up the breach we had made in
the temple wall pulled down about a dozen
cross-pieces of timber from the roof of our
tunnel so as to let the sand fall in and close
the passage against thieves and came
away leaving gulad on the spot in charge
ye understand put in allison we
never mistrusted gulad for a minute
never for a minute brice agreed
he was as enthusiastic as ourselves he
used to sit and talk with us in the evenings
displaying an intricate knowledge of bible
history he had theories of his own on a
number of things that have puzzled anti-
quarians he could read hieroglyphics
we used to give him worn and broken
inscriptions to decipher and he had a fair
average of success with them

aye we were well taken in said alli-
son we might ha known its not in
the naturesof an honest black man to be re-
ceiving letters from all quarters of the globe
brice laughed

  it never occurred to us to ask what his
voluminous correspondence was about now
and then wed see him writing until long
after midnight in his tent by candle-light
wed see his shadow on the side of the
tent ye understand put in allison
we respected his privacy exactly as if he
were a white man like ourselves

  well brice continued we left gulad
in charge and took the train for cairo
that meant a day on camel-back before
we reached the railway and a day and a
night in the train when we reached
cairo galbraith the official we had to see
was away and we had to wait three days
for him

  when galbraith returned-he arrived
at midnight and we kept him up all that
night talking though he was tired out-

res egaliter omnibus

it was nearly dark when we arrived
at appleton among bleak hills that had
been stripped of trees there were a few
truck gardens the inevitable roped cows
unthrifty-looking goats by the dozen and
of course pigs but nothing that resembled
farming it looked like one of those places
that import everything in tin cans milk
included and the empty cans made up most
of the scenery

  yet it wasnt such a small place it had
evidently spread during the war and had
begun to dwindle subsequently all the
straggling outlying parts of the town con-
sisted of mere shacks no longer painted and
inhabited exclusively by colored folk who
had come there for wartime wages and re-
mained because it was the easiest thing to
do there were rows and rows of empty
cottages and the white folks houses on a
hill at one end of the town wore a deserted
look as did most of the mines although the
pit-head gear in places appeared to be in
working order

  take it on the whole appleton was as
perfect a place as you could find for brew-
ing discontent you could hardly be happy
there if you tried what with falling wages
falling population falling houses trade
ambition everything mrs aintree
pointed out her home-a large frame house
on a hill-top standing in considerable
grounds but empty and i didnt wonder
that she lacked desire to live in it she said
that her late husband had controlled half
the mines in the place but the coal was low-
grade stuff and hardly paid for mining any
longer

  mrs aintree had engaged the town hall
ten days in advance and the place was

himself understood and laughed at her

i wont go so far as to pretend that
relations as between mrs aintree and
myself had been exactly cordial hitherto
they had not but up to the point of that
lodge meeting in appleton we had been able
to observe the amenities we had presented
to the world at large a reasonably amicable
front she detested and despised me and
while i did not overestimate her charms or
her integrity i was rather sorry for her so
we had managed to exist on the same
pullman car without exploding

  but to be made ridiculous was too much
i had committed the unforgivable offense
she hadnt a forgiving disposition at the
best of times but i had passed the rubicon
she could no longer look at me without glar-
ing could not even see me without mutter-
ing  could not frame polite words in answer
to the customary social salutations and on
top of all that she was imbecile enough to
suppose that i wasnt alert for her ven-
geance

  it is only the secret enemy who is dan-
gerous the man or woman who makes
faces at you or defies you openly is as easy
as a rattler to contend with for you have
had notice her lust for revenge was as
venomous as concentrated snake-juice but
she seemed to think herself possessed of
a fairy-book cloak that rendered her hatred
invisible

  a half-witted man would have realized

decided that they were unfortunate people

the men who wouldnt be led

                      by wm l mallabar

             charlie got his in a barroom scrap
             somewhere north of the line

              tom was doped on the barbary coast

               jack was crushed in a mine

               there isnt one of the old gang left
               but me-and im nearly dead

               the last of the crew-the crowd i knew

              the men who wouldnt be led

               were not so bad when you figure us out

              we played the game pretty square

              we always shared the good-and the bad

               and treated the worst of them fair

               it was just a kink in our brains i guess

             that made us hate home and a bed

              we had to get out and travel about

              the men who wouldnt be led

               we have left our bones on the desert plains
               and up on the yukon trail

              we have faced big odds laughed at men and gods

               but you never saw one of us quail

               to us a flag is only a rag

               but we fought for the hand that fed

              for the game-won or lost no count of the cost

              the men who wouldnt be led

               up on a trestle or down in a mine

               on the veld-in the hills-through the snow

                we have followed our choosings a will-o-wisp fire

              all we knew was-it forced us to go

              and now i am going-that last shot was straight

             no name-unidentified dead

               oh the tales that well tell when we gather in hell-

              the men who wouldnt be led

the modus opercmdi was as follows

  a dump-car was driven at a moderate
rate of speed to within a few yards of this
bumper the horse would then be pulled
sharply about automatically unhooking the
whiffletree chain from the car the car
would then proceed at its own momentum
until it struck the bumper the force of
such impact operating the mechanism
that dumped the load the horse would
then be rehooked to the dumped car and
the process repeated

  dynamite was quite familiar with the
details of this operation-much more so
in fact than was his driver whether the
powerful black had it in mind to show off
his capabilities before his new attendant or
whether his act was inspired by a pure love
of deviltry was a matter of individual
opinion at any rate notwithstanding
kingdons desperate attempts at control
the horse assumed complete charge of the

  so that the bonny continued to rock

  and the natives who plied up and down

  in these few days where the creeks were

mother nigers lap where the gurgling
rattle of complaint in their throats was
swiftly soothed into silence	

  whereupon their women donned their

n a certain day in the long ago
five gunmen descended from
surrounding hillsides and closed
in on henry madrigal cowman
who had assumed squatters rights in the
san andres mountains before government
surveyors had parceled out the country for
purposes of homesteading and purchase
madrigal fought a valorous but losing
battle drilling two of his assailants into
eternal silence before the combat reached
the hand-to-hand stage then he clubbed
his long-barreled six-shooter and rendered
two more temporarily helpless before a bul-
let nicked his valiant old heart

 madrigal lived long enough to demand
that some one look after his children-
a little boy and girl presumably waifs who
were known to be living with him they
had disappeared and the supposition was
that the killers had included them in their
slaughter but disposed of the bodies
ashamed of their inhumanity

  madrigal also during the last hours of
his life told sam barnhill owner of the
fying y in lurid and specific detail what
he thought of him

  then he died a martyr to the cause of
range freedom in traditions of the south-
west he still lives as a criterion of courage

  two of the gunmen of course required
nothing but burial for their services the
other pair apparently received what they
thought they were worth and left for parts
unknown

  zeigline the one killer who remained

on his feet when madrigal fell squatted on
the location until uncle sams engineers
platted plotted and surveyed the san
andres shortly thereafter title to the
madrigal place passed to barnhill-as
madrigal in his last moments had prophe-
sied it would zeigline received his pieces
of silver in live stock bearing a box x
brand-which analyzed was nothing more
or less than the madrigal m amplified
and doctored by the branding-iron

  even at that time barnhill was con-
trolling range factor his five thousand
head of cattle dotted the flats that spread
like a canopy at the eastern base of the san
andres madrigal had nested several
miles back in the mountains with a small
bunch of live stock this had made
madrigals western range boundary identi-
cal with the eastern limits of flying y
which was intolerable to barnhill who saw
in it a design to usurp his empire and steal
his cattle

  he had dealt with it accordingly vigor-
ously and expensively

  the old madrigal place thus became
one of the numerous flying y camps
later it was known as madrigal place
today it is referred to as madrigal

  after this experience barnhill looked to
his defenses determined never again to be
afflicted by nesters like many great
generals however he overlooked one strate-

dear mr barnhill

  this will introduce the t l cattle company
which respectfully requests the immediate removal
of all flying y cattle from the water known as
madrigal

  this land we are informed has long been con-
sidered flying y property but a survey made pre-
liminary to our purchase of same from the govern-
ment proves otherwise your holdings some forty
acres secured by patent are located about four
hundred yards south of the ground you claim

  our leases in addition to what we have acquired
by purchase fully protect our interest in the range
while of course your cattle have a perfect right to
graze on the forty acres you own the range will i
think prove too limited and dry for either comfort
to them or profit to you

   i will take this opportunity to make an appoint-
ment with you at the tedler hotel for the morning of
october  i will arrive on number four which is
due at  better make it about the time of my
arrival as a trainload of t l cattle will land in
tedler shortly after the passenger the stock will
be driven to madrigal immediately as the four-
hour ride from el paso will not necessitate their
being held for rest in the stock pens

yours very truly

louis bern manager

  how things had changed oksook was not
  an indian his was a parentage of a burly
  russian soldier and an eskimo squaw he
  had spent his fatherless youth and early
  manhood on the desolate salt marshes of the
  yukon delta what a terrible struggle for
  existence it had been searching the bliz-
  zard-swept floes for seal and walrus and
  crouching through the long winters in
  stinking igloos the hardships had forged
  him into a powerful sinewy hunter who
  threw a harpoon with accuracy and force

    then had come the gold-strike up the
  yukon at dawson thousands of white
  men came hurrying north to the island of
  st michael great river-boats were built
  to carry the passengers and freight up-river

oksook



the tananas disease and whisky had not
yet contaminated this sturdy tribe and
oksook found the lithe slender squaws
much to his liking they were not like the
fat greasy eskimo women on the coast

  with his new manners and air of bravado
picked up from the boisterous boat crews he
soon ingratiated himself into the favor of
the tribe cunning and patience inherited
from his slav father gained him fame and
prestige he came to be known among the
various tribes on the yukon and tanana
rivers as oksook the great medicine-man

  month after month year after year he sat
in his little cabin among his pots and bags
of herbs brewing muttering and sleeping
while the hunters brought him the fattest
salmon and sweetest moose livers in re-
turn for these favors oksook made charms
so that when the countless hordes of caribou
migrated north to their winter feeding-
grounds in the koyokuk they always crossed
the yukon near the village of the tananas
always it was his medicine that filled the
caches with fish therefore as he grew old
and fat oksook smiled and was happy

 but there came a summer when the
salmon changed the course of their run and
avoided the squeaking fish-wheels of the
tananas farther up the river the drying
racks of the kantishna tribe swayed under
their heavy loads of oily fish soon oksooks
tribesmen began to murmur and criticise
day after day he stewed and concocted
strong medicines but the dipping baskets
of the wheels splashed idly and brought up
nothing-a few small whitefish and gray-
ling when fall came the caribou followed
a new ridge north and crossed the river far
above the tananas

  the kantishnas laughed gleefully and
boasted of strong medicine as they traded
their fish and meat to the tananas for fox
and beaver-skins now came also a noisy
little missionary who told them that
oksooks medicine was fraud and oksook
was ruined

  thus it was that the old native became
the joke of the village and an object at
which the boys threw sticks and the squaws
scolded when he begged for bits of meat and
cast-off moccasins each year as he
starved and became more wretched the old
man grew more bitter and morose he had
long since ceased making medicine but
every morning left the village with its vici-
ous urchins and went down the trail to

tanana where he loitered about the saloons
and stores often he met white men who
had known him when he had been famous
and sometimes they gave him money and
whisky

  but this winter promised to be long and
hard and the old medicine-man felt uneasy
when at night he heard the malemiuts
mourn with their pointed noses toward the
green sky

  upon his arrival at the little cluster of

nor half-breeds could buy liquor over the

  the news of the fearful noises coming

on the indians in the village had spread

cine-man oksook found himself the object

  at first he took it good-naturedly smiling
sagely to himself but as the day wore on
and he drank more and more of his flask
his good humor changed to irritation and
anger which did not decrease the sport but
served rather to aggravate it to mockery
this continued more or less throughout the
day until evening came with its more boister-
ous gathering in the saloons he retorted
to his tormentors with snarls of wounded
vanity until at last unable to withstand any
longer the continuous attack he staggered
from a chair in the barroom where he had
been sitting and shouted frantically to his
revilers

  me show you me show you oksook
he make strong medicine he stop beeg
noise on bull island

  with this he stumbled out into the night
followed by a great hubbub of drunken jeers

lowed the remainder of his liquor and
crashed the bottle against the heavy logs

  even in his passion-befuddled mind he
realized that he had made a fool of himself
now because of his insane boast he would
be jeered and laughed at also among the
white men with the fumes of the poison-
ous whisky lashing him to a frenzy of des-
pair and anguish he bowed his head into
the storm and followed a heedless direction
which led him not up the trail to the village
but down on to the wind-scourged surface
of the river

  no one but a drunken eskimo could have
beaten his way up against that freezing
storm he followed no straightway course
but buffeted from side to side by the blasts
slipped and floundered among the troughs of
powdery crystals between the clean-swept
ice-ridges the worn webbing on his old
snow shoes soon parted leaving the useless
frames dangling unheeded about his knees
he did not feel the bitter winds nor suffer
from the stinging particles his mind para-
lyzed from the liquor served but one pur-
pose to impel him forward and forward

  but the tremendous heart action induced
by the stimulant began to fail the old man

gradually his limbs commenced to shake
and his pounding brain to cool as his
deadened faculties came back to conscious-
ness he became aware of a sinister blackness
close to his right it was the open water
by bull island with the immunity of a
sleep-walker he had been staggering along
its jagged edge

  for a time he stood peering vacantly out
over the murky expanse no recollection
of the terrifying mystery disturbed his
half-conscious study the waters seemed
strangely soft and inviting he felt an
overpowering desire to sink into their
soothing depths out of the biting winds
therb was something hypnotic about the
white flakes that silently sought the dark
surface to sink gently out of view

  lurching forward he stood swaying uncer-
tainly over the very rim as he watched
the river appeared suddenly to rise before
him out of the depths was emerging a
black glistening body from which a pair of
luminous eyes stared malevolently

  before he could draw back a gushing roar
shook the air beating into his ears an old
familiar sound of his youth a sound that
oksook knew and loved

  it was the old rallying cry of the whale-
hunt

  then for a moment he stood silent watch-
ing the black body as it skirted the rim
disappearing at length in the darkness in
spite of the intense cold a warm feeling of joy
diffused his benumbed frame once more
he would be oksook the great man a man
to be respected a man to be deferred to
he must capture the great fish this very
night before the secret be discovered by
any one else

  how the whale happened to be so far
from its natural habitat did not perplex
him his years as pilot on the lower river
told him that so preoccupied was he with
his plans that on his way back across the
river he did not feel the biting wind nor
mind the rough going in fact the storm
seemed to be diminishing in violence

  once in his cabin he hastily sought an
old bundle brought with him when he first
came among the tananas from it he drew
a wicked looking harpoon head to which
was attached a long heavy native whale-

line it was still in good condition as he
fitted a shaft into the barbed steel he
hummed a weird old hunting-chant of his
youth the days when he had been the best
harpooner of the tribe several times he
flourished the dangerous weapon at imagi-
nary objects and was surprized to find his
arm still retained its former skill then
arming himself with his rifle and ax he
started back to the opening

  the furious winds had dwindled to a few
spent whispers when oksook again reached
the place where he had seen the whale
overhead above the scudding clouds a flat
moon disk glanced palely through ragged
fringes a dim light was diffused over the
water but the whale was nowhere to be
seen

  without waiting however for it to ap-
pear oksook began to prepare for its cap-
ture about midway between the ex-
tremities of the opening he selected near
the edge a large ice chunk which he hewed
down to the semblance of a stump around
this he wound his line leaving enough free
that he might throw his harpoon a con-
siderable distance this done he laid his
rifle and ax near and awaited his oppor-
tunity long years of hunting on the ice
floes in the bering stood him in good stead
with eskimo patience he waited hour after
hour for the great creature to appear once
it rose with a high spout but it was far over
near the island and as it sank once more
oksook wondered how long it would be
before morning would bring the indians
and white men to share in the capture and
thus deprive him of the sole honor

  the malemiuts in the village had begun
their morning chorus when oksook again
noted the swirl in the water that betrayed
the presence of his prey it was rising near
the rim and on its way up-stream would
pass directly by closer and closer the
glistening back came gliding swiftly and
easily it would be an easy throw if his
arm did not fail him

  his first nervousness was passing off he
drew back his arm tensely leaned forward
and then hurled the harpoon into the black
form as it surged past the instantaneous
whip of the heavy line almost upset him but
he retained a hold on the snub end a
terrific splashing and floundering turned the
whole morning into confusion great waves
of bloody spray came lashing up on to the
ice he did not dare to let the line out

the water but this time it was shorter
again the enormous body grew still and

sure his catch oksook took his ax and sank

vertebrae

hundred feet the air was very bumpy
and even the heavy solid le pere was
bounced around in the air currents as it

  at two thousand feet spears flew over

parallel to it five miles farther on a fork
in the tracks the southern branch leading to
the gulf of mexico was additional veri-
fication that he was right so he settled back
comfortably in his seat

  usually flying was merely mechanical
with him there was no particular pleas-
ure in it-just sitting there until he got
where he wanted to go today however
here was a certain satisfaction in it that
vas unusual the swift rush of the air past
him was cool and comfortable in contrast
to the baking land below and as the le
pere hurled itself through the misty lower
edges of cloud after cloud the feel of the cool
moisture was the acme of physical delight

  the le pere was the pride of the school-
one of the only two of the type on donovan
field it was immeasurably faster than
any other ship on the field-it could fly
circles around a d h and left fokkers
s e s and even the powerful little 
spad hopelessly out of the running

  like a majority of the american flyers

anything but frail clumsy de havilands or
rebuilt british and german scout planes for
so long that flying renewed its joy for him
as he surveyed the trim strong wings
without dihedral or overhang and the solid
laminated fuselage so different from what
he had been accustomed to since leaving
france

whole-souled flyers delight in flying an

the motor and nosed down the strongly

go up to a hundred and eighty miles an

three thousand feet high the big
le pere boomed along the san an-
tonio road it was three-thirty
and according to cals story the robbers
had almost three hours start probably
about ninety miles reflected spears unless
something had happened saunders knew
every foot of the country and had said that
there were no side roads that the thieves
would be likely to take

  they came to the fork in the road and
sleepy guided the plane squarely down the
middle line between the two for fifteen
miles ahead the roads were not more than
ten miles apart the gap between them grad-
ually widening

  they had sighted only two cars driving
along the way they were traveling both
very small ones they had played safe
and dropped earthward for a close look
the country was not thickly settled and
such traffic as there was which was small
was toward gonzara and not away from it

  saunders was watching the right-hand
road and spears the left they had trav-
ersed about fifteen miles on a line between
the two when gib reached over and shook
the pilot he pointed down to a tiny speck
traveling along the road toward san
antonio

  sleepy nodded and nosed down he
did not believe it was their car he figured
that to be several miles ahead what he
did not figure on was the stop the desper-
adoes had made on a side road to chain mr
saunders securely in a deserted shack
where they had left him with sufficient food
and water to last three days

  they were a thousand feet high when they
caught the car and as he circled over it
sleepy noted that it was large and black
and traveling fast the country under-
neath was mostly mesquite although iso-
lated cleared spots could be seen

  the le pere under his handling was
skimming the tops of the trees several hun-
dred yards back of the car sleepy flew

parallel to the road as low as possible and
throttled the motor slightly as they shot
past there were several men in the car
that was certain

  gib a long-barreled rifle in his hand
leaned over the rear cockpit as far as he
could stretch trying his utmost to identify
some one in the car they flashed by so
fast however that they could come to no
conclusion

  spears turned the throttle full on and
circled back once more he could scarcely
believe that was the car and yet it might
be certainly the passengers had craned
their necks to the utmost to watch them
but that would be only natural for anybody
to do in view of the low flying he was doing

  on the next trip past the car the thieves
gave themselves away probably it was
the sight of gibs rifle and the fact that the
circling of the plane denoted suspicion as
the two flyers shot by their heads turned
as they strained their eyes toward the car
they saw two revolvers spit flame at them

  gibs face was alight with exultation
and his eyes flashed fire through the big
goggles the shots did not bother them
particularly-a fast moving plane is about
as difficult a target as there is extant

  sleepy pointed backward and gib nod-
ded the map showed that the road
was without cross-cuts for twenty miles-
by the time the car covered that they could
be back again after signaling the posse
coming behind

  with motor turning eighteen hundred
and speedmeter showing a hundred and
forty miles an hour the le pere sped back
over the trail it took less than fifteen
minutes to reach the three speeding auto-
mobiles that held the sheriff and his
deputies the le pere turned around
flew straight for a moment and then banked
steeply to the right it was the agreed-
upon signal to indicate which road the
bandits had taken

  the flyers wasted no time but went swift-
ly back on their way they found the car

every effort to get where they were going as
fast as might be practicable the le pere
passed it five hundred feet high and flew
on ahead the next town was fifteen miles

omething was standing in the
trellised moonlight beneath the
pine trees something half re-
vealed half shadow half seen
half guessed and it had not been there
a few seconds before being in england the
land of pheasant worship and the grouse
cult the shadow ought to have been a dog
or a fox-but it was not it looked like
neither of these there was something differ-
ent about it a suggestion of greater wild-
ness it was a little larger than a fox a little
smaller than a wolf and when it turned its
eyes shone green then it stepped into the
full light and became a coyote-a prairie
wolf

  the reason for so fierce a presence in so
tame a land came about as follows four
months before this coyote had struck a trap
in america-on the south fork of the stink-
ing water river to be exact one month

before he was touring england as part of a
traveling menagerie upon this night of
nights he had been fed by his keeper-who
was beautifully and unashamedly drunk

  they found the man asleep in the cage
next morning the key of the cage they
found on the floor but the coyote they
never found so much of this animals
history is known so much is public prop-
erty the rest that which follows is here
revealed for the first time it was told to
me by the wild folk and it is not given to all
to understand the language of the wild folk

  a thin high scream cut aslant the cathe-
dral silence of the pinewood a scream al-
most startlingly like that of a child and the
coyote froze he sank upon the grass
and was still eyes gleaming ears pressed
close back thin lean upper lip lifted a little
to display the cruel glistening fangs only
his flanks moved still heaving to the
the outcast



exertions of his ten mile removal from the
menagerie then quite suddenly came the
scream again and the coyote slid away on
his stomach

   he knew that cry the last protest against
fate of a terrified rabbit he came to a
hedge and peered through he saw a shad-
owy beast with a big bush-tail-something
like his own-dragging a kicking rabbit
toward the hedge red fox the coyote
noted to himself-and waited by which it
may be seen that he knew all about foxes

  reynard came on-knowing nothing in
his turn of coyotes-and slid into the hedge-
ditch with his struggling prey he preferred
to kill it here than in the open where its
pestilential squeal might attract any horror
-a keeper for instance

   g-r-r-r said the coyote a yard above
him in the tangle of hedge-roots and the fox
stiffened from head to foot he was fright-
ened that fox frightened no end for he
had never heard a growl like that before in
all his marauding life and a stoat that
was enviously watching the fox fell back-
ward down the bank of the ditch and fled
while a dry rustling flutter oerhead told
that the roosting birds had heard that
growl also and were afraid

   a head-almost wolf-like in its evil fierce-
ness almost fox-like in its leering cunning-
was thrust suddenly from out the matted

behind the head came the coyote not
slowly but like a brown streak the fox
was aware of a burning pain in his near
shoulder and he pivoted cringing back
among the damp dead leaves of the ditch-
forgetting the rabbit the rabbit had not
forgotten him however it forgot nothing

   it went or rather to be more exact it was

the rabbit must have known but if that

check-mated his foes he counted without
the coyote check it may have been but

  bunny had no illusions the drumming

   the fox was first of all aware of it a long

close to him- it had eyes aflame that
thing and he shied at sight of it as a fright-

followed a glint of fangs as the apparition

  having dined the coyote set off across
country at the long tireless wolf gallop
that will run down almost any four-footed
thing in the end he had no idea how far
that menagerie might pursue him and he
was taking no chances a wild creature
never willingly takes chances

  once-that was when he bee-lined over
a bare blunt-nosed hill-a line of hurdles
leaped up in front of him he took them
in his stride and fell among ewes many and
loud voiced ewes who rose up as one sheep
and ran about complaining in a full throated
chorus seeing that the poor outcasts one
notion at that time-possibly at all times-
was self-effacement this action on the ewes
part was unkind for one thing it caused
him to fly the opposite line of hurdles like
a bird and for another it awoke a long low
blue beast from nowhere special which fell
in dumbly at the coyotes heels the shep-
herd called it a bob-tailed sheep-dog but
it looked more like a silent devil in the light
of the cynical moon it took the coyote
half an hour to shake that speechless horror
off his track

  once also-that was when he was gingerly
rounding the corner of a cattle-muddied

  the coyote waited ten minutes after the

very swiftly with every hair along his back

the terror of the taint which had hung in the

hours

  like a hunted cat this wolfish beast that

hall of a thousand columns presently came
a long-drawn sigh echoing down the pillared
aisles and after the sigh a big brown bird
flapping like some gigantic moth that was
a long-horned owl and it flew without sound
as an owl should

  suddenly the coyote turned his head
something-some swift moving shadow-
was coming across the bog how any beast
heavier than a mouse could cross that quak-
ing abomination was past understanding
but the shadow proceeded leisurely and un-
flustered enough and it did not sink in-
stantly the coyote the outcast slid to a
heather patch on his belly and lay there
crouched his green glazed eyes fixed and
staring at the shadow

  the apparition came on bounding lightly
from tussock to tussock of deer-grass half
insolently half playfully it seemed toying
with the suffocating death that it only es-
caped by a hairs breadth at each fairy-like
bound

  the coyotes lips dripped as he watched
his eyes took on a crueler glow the thing
was a deer he knew the breed the slender
legs the arched hind-quarters the graceful
step as if the beast were walking on air
as a matter of fact she-it was a she-was
a roe-deer but this he did not know he
took her for a prong-horn antelope perhaps
or a white-tail deer-but no matter his
tactics would be the same

  she passed him-he had taken care to lie
well to leeward-quite close so close that
as she vanished like some spirit of the wood
beneath the trees he saw the transient
gleam of her white rump quite plainly for

a minute he waited-time enough for her to
penetrate the wood well out of sight

  then he dropped his head and with tail
slightly lifted settled into the long easy
wolf-gallop on her trail he must have
known by the scent how close he was-or
how far-for never once did he sight her or
blunder on to her heels in the pine gloom
nor was she once startled or aware of her
tracker thus nicely did he judge his pace

  as the trail broke from the wood and led
straight now as a die over the open moor the
pace increased the lone night wind whis-
pered in his ears and the night-hawks and
bats did their own small hunting above him
but he never lifted his head his eyes were
glued on the way ahead only his restless
ears took note of that which went on around

  once a fox halted in the middle of trailing
a rabbit to stare at this big wolfish beast
loping by with gleaming eyes and once
something which was not a fox-though it
was as big-stood in his path chattering
obscenely forcing him to go round that
must have been a badger

  finally-this after an exhibition of clever
windings twists and turns-the trail led
toa spruce firwood black as a well and
still as a well or death here was needed
great caution and the coyote slid belly flat
the whorls of the trees came down and
brushed the needle-carpeted ground diffi-
cult to avoid hard even to creep through
hard that is when even the faint sound of
brushed back branches would have ruined
all in that desert-like silence

  suddenly as if some cold hand had seized
him the coyote sank where he stood sank
and was still like one that is stricken sud-
denly by death in front was a clearing
no more than a gap shadowed as to its
center by one lone big spruce fir and
beneath that lonely shade stood the doe
and she had business with something that
was there she was anxious that deer
anxious and happy making odd little noises
in her throat-and beside her were two
more shadows

  then she stood out before the moon and
there followed her two fawns their great
beautiful eyes shining like stars dainty
spirits of the shades-sleek shining satin-
eared velvet-nosed dappled and light as
thistle-down upon legs slender as hazel
wands

  how long the coyote lay and watched that
wonder is not known some hours perhaps
adventure

he might have been a figurecarved out of
dark and light oak for all the signs of life
he gave only his eyes lived those green
orbs with the strange coldness of cruelty-
cruelty is assuredly a cold passion-ap-
parent in their depths

  at last a badger-clumsy shuffling
furtive and morose-passed in the gloom
he was in a hurry that beast and when a
badger is taken that way dawn is not far off
this the doe knew-or seemed to know
she vanished with her double charge slowly
under the pall-like shade of the giant and
isolated spruce and when she came out
again she was alone

  as a smoke puff fades so faded this elfin
like haunter of the woods the flash as of
a faintly gleaming lamp caused by her light
rump as she whisked away was all the indi-
cation a watcher would have had of her
departure

  the coyote followed a slinking evil form
in the gloom followed her to the edge of

  in five minutes he was back again by the
miniature clearing crouching motionless as

was no more than a shadow-which drifted
up from nowhere special behind him-
drifted and stopped

  the minutes dragged on painfully almost
as if the dead silence the stagnant air was
waiting for something to happen

a fox passed slinking and car-
mine tinted about the jaws a

wonderful dawn which comes after the gray
and is followed again by a day of gray rain

  suddenly the coyote moved sat up and
the shadow-the shadow was gone only
a gleam as of twin stars a flash as of a low
burning lamp hastily extinguished marked

cious as to whether it had really been a
shadow after all and half doubtful as to its
ever having been there at all this also
the coyote did not see

  then the wolfish beast moved forward-
almost flat he proceeded--and his caution
was evident at every careful creeping stride
there was still darkness under the great
spruce and for a moment he stood con-
fusedly then in a soft place just the
least noticeable spot exactly where they
would be most difficult to get at he saw the
fawns they were lying down but not
asleep they were looking not at him but
past him behind him at something that
was there-the shadow had come back
again

  a gleam of white fangs scintillated in the
growing light but there was no sound as the
coyote half rose and dived forward headlong
upon the helpless fawns they tumbled to
their feet they could run fairly well fast
enough to avoid a man but not a coyote
and they fled

  then without any kind of warning at all
the shadow took unto itself life it hurled
itself forward head down preceded by short
but rapier-sharp horns at the speed of a
powerfully shot arrow and its mark its
goal appeared to be the coyote it was a
full-grown roe-buck and of all the cloven-
hoofed ones the roe-buck is the least
troubled with fear and flurry

  the coyote was behind one of the fawns-
just about to deliver one of his quick chop-
ping bites on the flank in fact-when he
became aware of a small-sized whirlwind
hurtling down upon him from behind

  he realized his danger and pivoted upon
himself presenting a fanged and snarling
front in one and the same second so it
seemed being specially designed to do so
but in the next second he was removed quite
twelve feet by the sharpest pair of horns and
the most impetuous buck he had ever run
up against when he regained his feet-
which was practically the same moment as
he touched the ground-he was furious
for this buck was only small-no larger than
a pronghorn antelope and the wound in his
shoulder pained abominably but he had
made one blunder he had not reckoned
the outcast



upon the peculiar character of the roe-buck
-there are deer and deer and the roe-deer
is unique

  next moment the whirlwind was upon
him again the fury of the charge was ex-
traordinary and amazed the half-recovered
coyote he did not-being only an outcast
wolfling-stand up to it but skipped to one
side and delivered one of his chopping flank
bites a whirl of pine-needles followed for
the roe-buck can turn as can no other deer
and the coyote fell backward as the quick-
est way to avoid a hissiiig slash of those
terrible forked little horns

  thereafter things happened and hap-
pened quickly the coyote was no amateur
in the matter of keeping a whole skin by
agility and agility alone but the deer he
knew were not like unto this little fury he
leaped and side-leaped and sprung back-
ward pivoted on his tracks spun like a top
rolled from under the flying hoofs raced like
a greyhound and generally performed on an
average thirty acrobatic feats a minute
his foe however plunged and stabbed
jabbed and hacked stamped and tore and
raged and fumed and bucked and backed
and thrust and cut and slashed and
foamed like unto some four-footed fiend
the coyote might have been a magnet and
the buck a piece of steel so close did they
follow one another

  now a coyote unharried and unpressed
is many kinds of a coward but a coyote
cornered and hanging on to his life by his
whiskers is just any sort of a devil you like
to name this may be why at the end of
five minutes of this death dance there were
triangular gaps on the roe-buck-chiefly
about the flanks-where the flesh had been

removed in chunks on the other hand the
coyote was striped much as is a tiger only
the stripes were red not black

  followed a collision i dont know how it
happened one of the combatants must
have made a mistake somewhere any-
way they met it was a bundle that is all
you could say a huddle and it was not nice
to behold the needles flew all whither
and the earth followed suit which beast
was where it was impossible to say save
for the horrible low and continuous snarling
of the coyote there was no sound but one
knew that death hovered round that jumble
of legs and bodies

  then with startling suddenness it
ceased-the fight was over the roe-buck
was standing rocking in his tracks as a tree
rocks in a gale the coyote lay on his back
ten yards away and both were unpleasant
to behold were unclean to the sight

  slowly the coyote heaved on to his feet
and stood staring stupidly-himself rocking
to and fro-at his enemy then like a
broken thing-reminding one in a singular
way of some half-killed insect-he dragged
himself away into the red eye of the rising
sun a shocking nightmare of a beast a blot
on the beauty of the dawn and even as he
went the roe-buck toppled forward on his
nose and lay still-for ever

  as for the coyote he blundered drunkenly
half blinded and half choked straight
ahead to the open moor and its scattered
bogs and into a bog and under it for ever
and all time

  and that is why the dozens and dozens
of people who scoured the country for that
lost and outcast coyote never found him-
and never will

   our camp-fire came into being may   with our june issue and since then
its fire has never died down many have gathered about it and they are of all classes
and degrees high and low rich and poor adventurers and stay-at-homes and from all
parts of the earth some whose voices we used to know have taken the long trail
and are heard no more but they are still memories among us and new voices are
heard and welcomed

   we are drawn together by a common liking for the strong clean things of out-of-
doors for word from the earths far places for man in action instead of caged by cir-
cumstance the spiritof adventure lives in all men the rest is chance

   but something besides a common interest holds us together somehow a real com-
radeship has grown up among us men can not thus meet and talk together without
growing into friendlier relations many a time does one of us come to the rest for facts
and guidance many a close personal friendship has our camp-fire built up between
two men who had never met often has it proved an open sesame between strangers
in a far land

   perhaps our camp-fire is even a little more perhaps it is a bit of leaven working
gently among those of different station toward the fuller and more human understand-
ing and sympathy that will some day bring to man the real democracy and brotherhood
he seeks few indeed are the agencies that bring together on a friendly footing so many
and such great extremes as here and we are numbered by the hundred thousand now

    if you are come to our camp-fire for the first time and find you like the things we
like join us and find yourself very welcome there is no obligation except ordinary
manliness no forms or ceremonies no dues no officers no anything except men and
women gathered for interest and friendliness your desire to join makes you a member

a word from j d newsom in connec-
tion with his story in this issue

new york city

  the custom of allowing captives to live a fairly
free and independent life until their heads are
needed may sound queer but it is an actual fact
such men are allowed to marry into the tribe and
usually go to their deaths quite cheerfully when the
time comes their children however belong to the
mothers clan group or moiety and are allowed to
grow up under the tutelage of their maternal uncle
it is a rather quaint custom-j d newsom

to my relief and surprize my explana-
tion to camp-fire of our magazines
use of dashes instead of cusswords was
strongly backed up by nearly all the letters
that came in and there have been a lot of
these letters im glad i cant see my
way to any other course in the case of this
particular magazine and on the other hand

i dont like to follow any course to which
the majority of you are opposed

  but i forgot to cover one point two of
you raise this point vehemently in letters
to me and they are i think quite right
dashes for cusswords yes but when it
comes to dashes for god hell devil
etc used not as cusswords but in a per-
fectly legitimate way theres no need of
dashes yet our magazine has on various
occasions used them in such cases that
use of the dash is as offensive to me as it is
to any of the rest of you im agin  it
and always have been but there are seven
of us on the staff and while most of us have
been here for years there are changes now
and then as there are a few thousand
other things to watch out for this distinc-
tion in the use of dashes isnt always fol-
lowed or even clearly seen




adventure

  for that matter its often hard to draw
the line those of you who have read proof
know that style and consistency are often
hard to maintain on one side the line
youll get a series of cases each case lead-
ing logically on to the next demanding for
it the same treatment and all of a sudden
youll find youve crossed the line-and
done it logically too or stated the other
way youll put one case on one side the fine
later put a second case on the other side
and then find theres no logical distinction
between the two

  we have lots of arguments on the staff
over cussword cases when is a cussword
a cussword sounds easy to answer but
sometimes we dont find it so also as
stated already sometimes we just make
mistakes

  our intention is to use the word itself
whenever it is not plainly a cussword as
used in that particular instance some-
times well make mistakes or at least our
judgment will differ from yours just be
easy on us and credit us with good inten-
tions

  another thing in poetry well let em
ride even as cusswords unless they get too
dog-goned bad that isnt quite logical
perhaps but when the word is essential to
the rhyme it is pretty violent stuff to stick
in a dash

an interesting report from one of
 our comrades of the gentler sex regard-
ing daniel boones brother and his family
in general

  the name and fame of daniel boone are insepar-
ably connected with the history of kentucky the
old pioneer has been honored in many ways and at
the present time a fine statue of him clad in hunt-
ing garb coon-skin cap rifle in hand stands in
cherokee one of the most beautiful parks about
louisville

yf any of the descendants of daniel and his broth-
ers still live in the state the old boone home-
stead being located at boones landing about thirty
miles below the city on the ohio river and not far
distant is the site of an old mill built by the boones
at a place caeed booneport the descendants of
hiram boone a brother of daniel live at boones
landing and a member of the family the late
grandison boone used to relate that when a small
boy he saw daniel boone who stopped there on his
way to missouri to bid his kinsmen good-by
grandison boone had but a childish recollection of
the old trapper but his description of him tallies
exactly with that of others who saw him at that time
boone had blue or gray eyes and brown hair and at

the present time many of his lineal descendants have
the same while the collaterals are brunettes their
dark hair and eyes probably inherited from boones
wife who was a bryan

t have been present at many of the family

  gatherings of the boones which country-bred
folks would call dinings early in the day the
members of the various families would begin to ar-
rive men women and children horses mules and
hound dogs the squawking of chickens would be
heard huge baskets of vegetables would be borne in
from the garden long rows of cakes were outspread
in the summer kitchen while in the inner kitchen
where the chefs of the household were on duty the
most luscious odors would fill the air city folks
would stand aghast at the generous supplies of food
prepared and its infinite variety

  meanwhile the men sat under the trees discussing
crops and politics over tall glasses of cold mint
juleps some chewed tobacco some smoked reeky
pipes while the horses and mules taken out of the
traces browsed contentedly close by the hounds
tumbled in the grass with the youngsters or loafed
about the kitchen door slobbering in delicious an-
ticipation of the feast

  when dinner was served all the guests sat about
a long table loaded to the guards with eatables
fried chicken rosy old country-cured ham chicken
and dumplings corn pudding field beans and bacon
bacon and greens tomatoes raw and cooked butter
beans spoon com bread rhubarb heaps of snowy
mashed potatoes buttermilk and hot coffee pie
cake preserves of all kinds pickles all the riches of
field garden and orchard were outspread while the
women of the immediate household waited on the
table and purged their guests to help themselves
bountifully-uncle hiram you must take some
more cake cousin squire i want you to sample
my cornbread and aunt becky i made this
lemon pie after your own recipe

jphe conversation was boone from post to finish
all the happenings in the family births mar-
riages and deaths old stories were revived par-
ticularly one to the effect that when the state of
kentucky brought the remains of daniel boone and
his wife back from missouri the real bodies were
never given up but weights placed in the caskets
so that the coffins buried with so much pomp at
frankfort hold no honored dust but as most of the
kentuckians know no better they are perfectly
satisfied another story revived and retold was
that of daniels brother squire boone who it is
stated by historians was killed by the indians

  the family indignantly disclaim this statement and
declare that he settled in indiana on the ohio near
boone township and there raised his family there
was a small cave near his home and on the walls of
this cave squire carved religious and worldly-wise
sentences one of the former ran i sit and sing
my souls salvation one of the latter was to the
effect beware of intentions for fear of preven-
tions possibly intimating that it was foolish to tell
what you intended doing last some one frustrate
your plans

  when old squire felt his end was near he got his
coffin and selected the cave for a place of burial
he made his three sons promise solemnly to place
his remains there sit up with them for three days
and nights then close up the entrance forever
the camp-fire



this they did and for many years the old man re-
mained unmolested in his rocky sepulchre

qome time in the s--purchased a farm

  on the kentucky side of the river below louis-
ville and it was there that he died on a black storm-

swept night his son-- a thoughtless

rollicking young man spent some time on this farm
having for his companion a member of one of the
oldest and most aristocratic families in the state
one day these two accompanied by a negro servant
and their hunting dogs crossed the river in a skiff
and went hunting in the indiana woods during
the trip they imbibed freely from their hunting-
flasks and after wandering over a big stretch of
country finally stumbled on the boone cave

  i have never heard whether they knew the story
of this cave or not anyhow in a spirit of reckless-
ness they broke open the rude entrance dragged
the skeleton of old squire from its resting place and
scattered the bones about everywhere it is whis-
pered to-day that a distinguished member of the
boone family now living has one of the leg-bones
of old squire though he can never be persuaded
to admit the fact even to his own household

  -and his gay companion finally withdrew to

their boat and the negro rowed them back to the
kentucky shore fully convinced in his superstitious
mind that the party was damned forever it is
certainly true that -- had persistent bad luck
after this escapade and died a violent death while
the boones regarded his taking off in the light of a
retributive justice

  the waters of the ohio still glide peacefully past
the cave the forests have disappeared and prosper-
ous settlements sprung up close by but the memory
of old squire boone lives immortal in the traditions
of his descendants and those of his brothers one
and all they snap their fingers at the historians and
their story that the squire was killed by indians
and declare that he died peacefully in his bed wear-
ing his scalp to the last-elvira millie slaugh-

ollowing camp-fire custom leon-
ard h nason rises and introduces
himself on the occasion of his first story in
our magazine

chicago

  so now i am come to take a place at the camp-
fire to meet with other read dr s adventurers and
authors the first i have ever been the second
some little time and the third have but now be-
come hence this standing up and this bow and this
present writing for all that i am of tender years
i have been with the most of those about this fire
what time they were in the far places of the world
upon their different occasions he that went down
to the sea and reefed topsails in the gale and passed
the weather earing i have been with him albeit i
stayed in on the yard in the bunt and clung to the
jack-stay with both hands for which i was beaten
about the ears by the second mate he that hath
been to the spanish main and on the beach thereof
i have been with him and have been cast out of the
american consulate whither i had gone for food
on my ear and gone to the british and been be-
trayed by my accent and then sought a missionary
and been given the grubstake that my own govern-

ment had denied me what so cheap in the central
americas as the life of a yankee

  those among you that know the feel of a squee-
gee and a marines boot and the joy of smuggled
cigarets in the brig and no tables down for days
and a tompion lost in a seaway with the north at-
lantic coming in through the breech-block like a
shower-bath i have been with you and have pol-
ished brightwork and seen the world through a port-
hole and strained shellac through bread for the alco-
hol therein like the saltiest shell-back among you

  and last i was with those who went to war hos-
pital huit chevaux replacement camp mps ship-
wreck vin ordinaire submarines i knew them all
from mezy to montfaucon i went i and my com-
rades of the rock of the marne with our battle cry
of when do we eat on our lips two bites of the
dust i took one at mont st pere and one in the
argonne which last was near to being the death of
me and then at last i was cast away on fire island
with the northern pacific and beat a watery grave
by a pigs whisper

  so much for the past i have hung up my sword
and made my old tin derby into a flower-pot and
given myself in marriage and will henceforth live
in peace and so to my grave to which i expect to
go in a hearse and without boots it is in my mind
that this can not be fulfilled for it hath been said of
me that i was bom to be hanged

  now then greeting to my new comrades of the
camp-fire and to the many gallant gentlemen
and true adventurers that i have met upon the
road to whom these words may come greeting
may they read my efforts with a kindly and not too
critical an eye how-leonard h nason

    the lay of the tropical tramp

weve tramped round the earth from twenty north
to twenty or more degrees south-

 weve lived in the gilded lobster halls

and weve lived from hand to mouth-

weve struck old london when flush with coin

and roamed through new yorks streets-

but weve always hit the tropical trail

when we find our bank rolls cease

weve drunk all drinks from pommery sec
to pulque and white label-

we have smoked dried grass and poros vile

or perfectos fine when able-

weve borne mosquitoes chigoes and fleas

screw worms have claimed attention-

malaria ague and yellow jack

and diseases that need no mention

but even though we parch and bake

theres joys to balance the scale-

a pair of dark eyes at every place

and good men along the trail

men with stories wild and strange

yarns that beat creation-

sweet-scented liars from every clime

drifts from every nation

tropical tramps tropical bums

tropical sour-doughs all-

we hit the pike on the tropical hike

 and expect to til we fall

through chapparal mesquite bamboo and palm
til the sunset of life doth fade-

well tramp till were lost neath the southern
cross

and get put to bed with a spade

hphe english coronation stone in westminster

hthese arc dangerous days in which to say how
much you believe and why its so easy to be

stition seem to be almost as confused as they ever

prove neither more nor less no mans opinion
or say-so is really proof of anything so in answer-
ing the question as to whether the ark of the cov-
enant if rediscovered would kill whoever touched it
i must be understood to be stating my own opinion
and nothing more

  i believe that it did kill any unauthorized person

not kill anybody now although it could be made to

san francisco

  ive justfinished the june th adventure which
contains mr w e bessons inquiry regardingethe
famous rope trick of the indian jugglers if a bit
of dope drawn from memory and not capable of sub-
stantiation at this time is admissable ill unburden
my chest of the account of my own encounter with
this yarn

come ten years ago i got hold of a book of travel
-one of mayne reids i think-in which was
a description of the trick as follows

  a fakir and a boy appeared in the streets of cal-
cutta  carrying a large covered basket when a
crowd had gathered the fakir produced from the
basket a rope and a big knife the rope he coiled
and heaved upward and one end went out of sight
and stayed while he held the bitter end in his
hand

  the boy at a signal from his master climbed the
rope hand-over-hand and disappeared a few mo-
ments later the fakir called for the boy to come
down but received no answer he called several
times and at last apparently irritated by the boys
silence placed the knife between his teeth and him-
self ascended the rope disappeared from above
the camp-fire	

came a blood-curdling yell then in rapid sequence
the severed arms legs head of the boy hurtled to
the ground followed finally by the hacked trunk
the fakir descended rapidly his knife dripping
blood he gathered together the pieces of the body
and placed them in the basket covering it again
after a mumbled incantation he opened the basket
and out stepped the boy unhurtl

  hats the account i read-and wondered over

    but a little later-in the same year-i came
upon a description of the trick in some magazine
it purported to be an expose of the trick

  an englishman who had heard of the feat who
was a dabbler in mesmerism and hypnotism waited
for a fakir to appear one came and the theater
was a street lined with blank walls-so that none
but the immediate assembly which was to a man
under the fakirs influence might see-and the
englishman with a camera found a hole in the wall
behind the fakir he took picture after picture and
when the fakir had finished and gone questioned
as many of the spectators as possible without ex-
ception these described the performance much as it
is described above but the pictures covering
every moment of the performance revealed only
the crowd gaping fixedly upward while the fakir
and his boy-assistant stood quietly by the basket on
the ground

  mesmerism of the spectators en masse was the
explanation advanced by the englishman he was
not susceptible to mesmeric influence he stated and
so was the only person to witness the performance
who saw just what he saw and not what the fakir
made him believe he saw

t wish i had the names of the book and maga-
zine from which the above are taken but
there was no reason at time of reading to think of this
point and i made no notes but id be willing to
take oath that ive given here the essence of the two
accounts they made a marked impression upon me
and my memory im told is inconveniently long

  whether or not the expose of the trick was au-
thentic i would like to learn perhaps both ac-
counts are mere travelers tales on a par with herod-
otus account of the crocodiles tears squien sabe
at any rate i know that some where in the books is
mention of this particular trick and what i saw
surely others of camp-fire saw also some of the
experts on mesmerism might also be able to say
whether or not such group-mesmerism is possible-
eugene cunningham

our camp-fire stations are spreading
steadily over the map but plenty of
room exists for more help make them grow

a station may be in any shop horrte or
other reputable place tne only require-
ments are that a station shall display the
regular station sign provide a box or drawer
for mail to be called for and preserve the
register book

  no responsibility for mai is assumed by anybody the
station merely uses ordinary care entries in register to be
confined to name or serial number route destination
permanent address and such other brief notes or remarks as
desired each station can impose its own limit on space to
be used registers become permanent property of station
signs remain property of this magazine so that if there is
due cause of complaint from members a station can be
discontinued by withdrawing sign

  a station bulletin board is strongly to be recommended
as almost necessary on it travelers can leave tips as to
conditions of trails etc resident members can post their
names and addresses such hospitality as they care to offer
calls for any travelers who are familiar with countries these
residents once knew calls for particular men if they happen
that way etc notices or tips about lecal facilities and con-
ditions letters to resident members can be posted on this
bulietin-board

  any one who wishes is a member of camp-fire and
therefore entitled to the above station privileges subject to
the keepers discretion those offering hospitality of any
kind do so on their own responsibility and at their own risk
and can therefore make any discriminations they see fit
traveling members will naturally be expected to remember
that they are merely guests and act accordingly

  keepers answer letters only if they wish for local
information write ask adventure 

   a station may offer only the required register and mail
facilities or enlarge its scope to any degree it pleases its
possibilities as headquarters for a local club or resident
camp-fire members are excellent

   the only connection between a station and this magazine
is that stated above and a keeper is in no other way re-
sponsible to this magazine nor representative of it

 lyty j mation on outdoor life and activities everywhere and
 aduthtir upon the various commodities required therein con-
 ducted for adventure magazine by our staff of experts

q

k uestions should be sent not to this office but direct to the
expert in charge of the section in whose field it falls so
that service may be as prompt as possible he will answer you
by mail direct but he will also send to us a copy of each question and
answer and from these we shall select those of most general interest
and publish them each issue in this department thus making it itself
an exceedingly valuable standing source of practical information
unless otherwise requested inquirers name and town are printed with
question street numbers not given

    when you ask for general information on a given district or subject
the expert may give you some valuable general pointers and refer you
to books or to local or special sources of information

    our experts will in all cases answer to the best of their ability using
their own discretion in all matters pertaining to their sections
subject only to our general rules for ask adventure but neither they nor the magazine assumes any
responsibility beyond the moral one of trying to do the best that is possible these experts have been
chosen by us not only for their knowledge and experience but with an eye to their integrity and reliability
we have emphatically assured each of them that his advice or information is not to be affected in any
way by whether a given commodity is or is not advertised in this magazine

	service except our assaying and oil services free to anybody provided self-addressed
   envelop and full postage not attached are enclosed correspondents writing to or from
   foreign countries will please enclose international reply coupons purchasable at any
   post-office and exchangeable for stamps of any country in the international postal
   union

	send each question direct to the expert in charge of the particular department whole
   field covers it he will reply by mail do not send questions to this magazine

	no reply will be made to requests for partners for financial backing or for chances to
   join expeditions ask adventure covers business and work opportunities but only
   if they are outdoor activities and only in the way of general data and advice it is in
   no sense an employment bureau

	make your questions definite and specific state exactly your wants qualifications
   and intentions explain your case sufficiently to guide the expert you question

	send no question until you have read very carefully the exact ground covered by

two-bladed knives

a wicked addition to anybodys arma-
ment looks like

  question-i note in ask adventure that you
kindly offer to give information in regard to antique
edged weapons and i am presuming to impose
upon your good nature

  i have a double-bladed weapon which was willed
to me by a friend i did not know that he owned it
and i have no idea where he got it

  it is  inches long the blades are  inch wide
at the broadest place both blades are thin and
sharp on both edges

  both sides of both blades are covered with in-
scriptions which are raised above the surface
evidently from the appearance of the metal the
background was etched away




adventure

  the surface of the steel was rusted when the
weapon came to me and from its appearance i do
not think it was ever highly polished

  the handle between the two blades is of a green-
ish-white bone the like of which i have never seen
before it is carefully finished but not highly
polished on each side of the handle is engraved
a row of circles with a dot in the center about one-
fourth of an inch in diameter

  the sheaths are evidently of rawhide of some
sort covered with what looks like cheetah skin-
tawny yellow fur with small black spots this
fur though evidently very old and much worn
has never been attacked by moths

  the blades look very old and are of primitive
workmanship though the lette-ng is bold and
free and drawn by no uncertain hand if you will
examine the black-lead tracing which i am enclosing
you will see that the inscription is the repetition of
one word over and over again i do not know the
language though it looks something like turkish
or possibly jagatai or even persian

  the surfacing of the blades is much like that of
a turkish boarding-cutlas now in my possession
which was presented to my father by gen grant

  i would much like tojknow your opinion on the
nationality of this weapon and the probable limiting
dates of its manufacture also the kind of fighting
it was used in and against what sort of weapon it
was used in defense

  ancient weapons interest me much if i know
something of their history and method of use in
offense and defense collecting them is a fine avoca-
tion in which i understand you are an adept

  i have a straight double-edged sword on one side
of which is engraved in fifteenth-century spanish
draw me not without cause and on the other
side sheath me not without honor do you
know if the engraving of mottoes was common on
spanish arms of that period have you ever seen
the above mottoes on a sword-blade and if so of
what country and period

  thank you much for any information you can
give on the above matters

  drop in and see me whenever you have nothing
better to do my office is near south station

  stamped self-addressed envelop enclosed-
allan v garratt boston mass
