just floatin

wish i could slough this magazine
     job for six months and go along

  question-i want information on the following
points

  it is understood that the following questions
apply to boats without propelling force other than
oars or sweeps

  is the middle of september too late to leave
pittsburgh and float down to new orleans

  if so would it be advisable to start from st
louis

  what is the approximate time to float from pitts-
burgh to cairo floating days only

  same from st louis to new orleans

  is it safe to float nights with lights but with no
watch

  will current boards keep a floating boat in the
channel-edward french m d boston
mass

  answer by mr spears-you could leave pitts-
burgh in september and make the gulf by tripping
the ohio but id have my doubts about the ohio
river without a little power-an outboard motor
say there are long pools above the ohio river
dams and youd have to lock through about forty
of the dams which enable steamers to run in low-
water periods st louis would be better for then
ask adventure



you have the river current all the way down four
to seven miles an hour

  a days float would be about forty miles you
would be able to drop down about four days a week
on the average working down comfortably say
new orleans in about seven or eight weeks you
could jam through in less time i took all winter-
seven months-to make this trip down but i just
sauntered along went over to st francis river
for  miles and down chaffelli

  i shouldnt advise night floating without a watch
it is done by old river-men on a falling stage of the
river which keeps the boat in mid-stream a rising
stage throwing the boat to the bank you might
meet a fleet of tow barges and be rolled under them
which happens i floated nights when the day-
winds prevailed but kept watch its an eery
experience

  never heard of any one using current boards or
sea-anchors except that in winds shanty-boaters
in a hurry run into the drift and make fast and are
towed down this is dangerous if the driftwood is
swirling and the boat is caught in a squeeze

  im enclosing a slip thatll help you a little
come again if im needed

  if you dont want an answer enough to
enclose full return postage to carry it
you dont -want it

        stock-raising in australia
pine opportunities there still

  question-having seen your address in ask
adventure columns of adventure magazine as one
who answers queries regarding australia and its
resources i am taking the privilege of writing you

  i am interested in the stock industry in australia
have had ten years experience in ranch life in
north dakota and montana u s a i would like
to get in touch with some of the leading stockmen
of your country can furnish reference of any kind
regarding character and ability-john w pat-
terson fort mills philippine islands

  answer by mr goldie-stock-raising land in
australia is usually divided into first second and
third class grades as in all other new countries the
richer soil was the first to be occupied and worked to
advantage in many districts it proved that only
the first class would pay under the then existing cir-
cumstances but as conditions impuved second-
class country became as desirable as the first-class
hadoriginally been then the third-class came into
occupation and in turn proved profitable because it
was secured at low values and had been brought
within easy communication with markets

  in the most closely settled states such as victoria
and tasmania the greater extent of the land is held
under freehold and the newcomer who desires to
secure property will find it best to buy an improved
place or better still to purchase some good grazing-
land which is still subjectto considerable improve-
ment if he has moderate means the buyer can
make a fair profit on his capital while he continues
to increase the value of his holding

  well-fenced and watered sheep or cattle country
provided with a homestead may be bought in these
states at from  to  pounds per acre according to

its carrying capacity while properties in first-class
order and capable of grazing two sheep to the acre
cost considerably more usually these are compara-
tively small and a good deal of cultivation is carried
on by the holders it is on such places as these
however that a skilful stock-breeder may make
money and accumulate property

  like all good things the best of the grazing-
estates in tasmania and victoria must be paid for
but once established and with the right class f
stock in the paddocks the enterprising breeder from
the old country soon makes good on his purchase

  what can be accomplished on small sheep-
holdings has been demonstrated by the merino
sheep-breeders of midland tasmania here many
of the old famihes have carried on flocks of the
finest merino for many years they raise thousands
of stud sheep and send them to the mainland for sale

  the tasmanian sheep were introduced into hun-
dreds of the largest flocks in the other states and
sheep-breeding in australia owes much to the group
of accomplished breeders in the little island flocks
of one thousand to two thousand sheep kept up to
a high standard have been sufficient to maintain
their owners in the position of small pastoral kings
in other parts of the island splendid herds of cattle
have been reared and maintained on small holdings
and have kept prosperous families on the land

   in victoria there is still some leased country and
there are thousands of acres of grazing-land that
offer opportunities to the buyer who has pastoral
instincts and prefers stock-keeping to the more
arduous agricultural operations

  north from victoria on the murray river into
the southern pastoral districts of new south wales
a great extent of sheep country stretches away west
and east into the mountains which stand back from
the coast throughout this wonderful stretch of
pasturage there are great productive grazing-
properties which carry from one sheep to an acre to
three sheep to two acres the newcomer in his
search for land will find large estates varying in
value from  to  pounds per acre

  provided that he is in a position to finance the
purchase of land and stock he can not fail with good
management to make any of these properties pay
very handsomely there are also chances of secur-
ing land on lease from the government and others
of acquiring a partly improved portion of a large
run which is being cut up by the owners or subdi-
vided after resumption by the state

  the stock-breeder from older countries finds con-
ditions in australia almost completely the reverse
of those to which he has been accustomed all
animals live in the open air in this wonderful climate
the year round with the exception of certain stock
such as special studs working horses and perhaps
the house cows all the animals feed solely on the
sweet pastures of the country

  the newcomer wonders at what he considers to be
the insufficient steading about the country homes in
australia it is soon explained to him that no build-
ings are needed for housing animals in the winter
and this mainly accounts for the difference in the
size of a live-stock establishment in australia and
one in a cold country

   as a matter of course the working conditions also
differ to a considerable extent much less labor is
required to work the same number of cattle or sheep
and most of the work is in the open paddocks
a direct consequence of this is that one man can
i

adventure

attendto a large number of animals there are
yearly operations such as lamb-marking and shear-
ing and cattle-mustering and branding while in
between the animals are worked from paddock to
paddock according to the condition of the pas-
tures

  fats come on much more irregularly than in
a country where hand feeding is universal but stock
fatten very quickly on the rapid flushes of grass and
herbage which come in good seasons the animals
make amazing progress from birth to maturity
unless they get a very severe check the system is
thus almost as simple as that on the cattle ranches
of texas and the argentine

  most stock-owners in australia stock lightly be-
cause of the variations in the seasons as a rule
when the pastures are very good the broad acres
could carry many more animals than are grazed
upon them but the long-headed stockmen are
satisfied to keep them in good condition and con-
serve the grass for recurring periods of dry weather

  on his tours of inspection which are usually
necessary before he can settle down the land-seeker
acquires a great deal of knowledge of the country
which is essential to his career as a pastoralist he
soon learns to distinguish sheep from cattle country
and picks up many wrinkles on the management of
all classes of stock in australia information on the
value of water conservation is impressed upon him

  except in western australia where on extensive
pastoral areas scrub and small timber are necessary
to augment the carrying capacity of the country he
sees the advantage gained by killing the exhausting
green timber and cleaning up the surface of the land
as the dry timber falls the experienced australian
pastoralist will take him in hand on train and
motorcar journeys and freely impart to him the
tips he has gained in years of pioneering in the
country

  having at last made his selection and secured his
property which may be  or  acres in
extent according to his means his ambitions and
the class of country he prefers his first year will be
almost solely devoted to making decisions as to the
best means of working his place and of following such
operations as he can compass if he has purchased
an improved property he will learn how to use the
paddocks water supply and other improvements on
the best scheme for keeping his stock in condition
as a general rule he will discover that to make the
best of his holding he needs to complete theimprove-
ments

  starting on an improved place however the new
owner has a great advantage in being able to work his
stock to immediate profit he can devote most of
his time and his resources to the actual management
of his sheep or cattle and the place begins to produce
for him from the jump

  on the other hand if he begins on partially im-
proved property the principal improvement is likely
to be the killing of most of the timber and the erec-
tion of part of a ring fence he may secure a tank
or a well or two but on the other hand the place
may at first be-waterless so the new settler must
hustle into fencing tank or well sinking and the
erection of a house and some steading the princi-
pal advantage he has in this country over the man
who takes up virgin acres is that the native grass
and herbage are well established because the timber
has been killed in part and these respond to a mod-
erate rainfall with a lively growth of healthy feed

  therefore the place can be stocked up immedi-
ately and alambing and a clip of wool may be
expected in the first year of occupation

  the operations in making a home in subdividing
the holding securing a water supply breaking up
a paddock with a plow and raising wheat or oats or
hay and grain for his horses establishing a small
herd of milkers for the family supply of milk and
butter will be simple enough to an experienced
stockman coming from the old country as the
most urgent jobs are completed a vegetable garden
and a small orchard are added to the supplying
areas when the new man in australia reaches this
stage he is beginning to settle down to the comfort-
able life of a landed proprietor

  among the leading stock agents of this country
are dalgety  co ltd sydney the cooperative
wool and produce co ltd sydney the new
zealand loan and mercantile agency co ltd
with head offices in new zealand and branches all
over australia chisholm  co sydney cramsey
 lithbridge ltd sydney hill clark  co
sydney and winchcombe carson ltd sydney
there are many others but if you apply to any of
these firms you will no doubt get all the information
you require regarding your chances in this industry
in australia

d	c they make a canal-boat that only draws

fishing stocked ponds

the more you preserve fish in order to
angle for em the wiser they get
brother thompson has doped out a way to
fool old dame instinct but my guess is that
the dodge wont work more than a few years
in any one pond

  question-would like to have your advice on
the best method and kind of baits to use for catching
old stock of bass in small lake or pond these
ponds were stocked say twelve or fifteen years ago
with bass for a few seasons they were very easy
to catch but for the last four or five years they have
been refusing to strike at either live bait or artificial
bait of almost every variety

  these fish can be seen late of an afternoon close
in to the shore but can not be persuaded to strike
at any lure-- walton ky

  answer by mr thompson-i have had the same
experience as you with stocked ponds but finally
got bass striking as fish grow up in a pond i
am sure they feel the vibration from a mans move-
ments on shore also the small fish that feed in
shoal water scare quickly at the presence of man

and somehow or other transmit this to the other
fish by their actions of getting into deep water

   i have studied this subject a great deal and finally
began wading i got to where i wanted to cast
and after getting in the water never made any
movement whatever for a long time i am sure
fish do not see you when you are waist deep in water
as they do when you are on shore i used the light-
est artificial lures with the grooved heads that i
could get they went deep on fast reeling and
brought out many a big fellow

  when using a fly rod often i had good results
from a bucktail and an  spinner the smallest size
i used many times with success the little fiy-rod
wooden plugs and one of the best methods is to
attach a dropper fly as usual and carry along the
little plug as trailer this looks like a minnow
chasing a fly and very few bass can resist it many
ponds like yours are fine for late evening and night
fishing the best killers are bass bugs with fly
rod and any surface minnow with it reel slowly
and you will have a fine thrill when they strike

  question-i am a workingman and filled up and

south sea islands are the subjects of these questions

   do you believe that there is a chance for a man
that worked from south america to alaska for his
living to make it there as a native

  can a man get land there enough to live on
i do not want a plantation it would mean grinding
again

to to take a look for that home

   where can i get maps and literature on the south
sea islands

gus hamil ketchikan alaska

  answer by mr charles brown jr-you are
a workingman you say you are fed up with the
year-long grind and anxiety of modem civilization
your only escape lies in spreading sail for the warm
blue waters of polynesia you believe

   you see an island valley with a little grass house
hidden away in the green depths of the jungle all
about it are fruits and vegetables and flowers and
at the foot of the valley is a lagoon sparkling
through long hours of sun always it will yield you
the choicest sea-foods never never will it fail

   far up in the cool of the valley run wild chickens
and hogs and goats and cattle-yours all yours for
the killing you have but to call the happy laughing
islanders and they lead you to the hunt at dusk
ask adventure v



you return heavily laden to partake of the
kill

  then comes the moonlight orange-colored and
all-pervading and with it the laughter and the
singing of pretty brown island girls so that you for-
get almost entirely the loneliness and desolation of
the bleak land in which you were a prisoner of cir-
cumstances

  such is the picture your unhappy mind has
painted but oh my fellow creature out on the
beaches of polynesia life can be as cruel as a prohi-
bition officer especially to the man who in the
twilight of his years wants to turn native

  now mark when a happy reckless south sea
vagabond holding my own on breadfruit and fish
and sleeping out on the decks of becalmed island
schooners i met up with all manner of beach-comb-
ers men that had tired of civilization in much the
same manner as you have done this legion is
knocking about with but one regret ever they
curse the hour they turned their backs on the- home
of their fathers for an uncertain struggle under the
southern cross and always they whispered to me
their one hope spoke of the day when they would
drift back to the lands so far above the line oh
my friend the stories i could write of these breth-
ren of the beach

   i do not want you to become one of that legion
nor do i want to think of your plunging lower than
a native you must understand that to a native a
beach-comber or white man that has gone native is
less than the coral over which the island dogs run

   can you get enough land to live on yes if you
have money-several hundred dollars for in those
island valleys cash has now become a medium of
exchange even amongst the hospitable natives

  how much capital would you think is needed
to get a start to live as a native not as a capitalist
why when a man goes native he does so because
his purse is nothing more than a rag if he had
capital he would never turn to that life only
when his resources fail when he can not again get on
his two feet does he accept from the natives what-
ever they see fit to throw to him indeed i have
stripped the south sea of its glamour im cruel
am i not

  the enclosed form cash needed by all guests
on tahiti island tells more about the thing i am
driving at

   frederick obrien has written two very readable

city

other waterproofing recipes

ywo of em both simple enough too

  question-i take your name from adventure as
being the person to whom to write with reference to
camping etc i would appreciate it if you would
send me a good recipe for waterproofing a tent-
c sulling new york city

  answer by dr hathome-there are various
methods for waterproofing tents one of the best is
a preparation consisting of sugar of lead and alum
equal parts say about a pound of each to a gallon
or more of tepid water when dissolved put tent in
soak in the solution and when thoroughly saturated
hang up and dry this will make a tent fairly water-
proof mildew-proof and to a certain extent fireproof

  another way is to dissolve paraffin in gasoline
out of doors then paint the preparation on the can-
vas this will make a tent waterproof but is not so
easily applied and a tent is more likelyto catch fire
from sparks from a camp-fire

qne of the most interesting contributions to this
department since i took hold of it is a letter
from a dear old lady seventy-six years of age who

the poem-they sure go together

              edgetts landing albert county
              new brunswick canada

am sending you an old song which may be called
a sea ditty-a doggerel affair in these days it was
composed about the time of happening  as
my father learned it in the year  when he went
to new york a lad of seventeen he used to sing
it along with others when i was a little girl until i
learned it from hearing him i am now an old wo-
man of seventy-six and probably the only one in the
world who knows this song wish i could send you
the tune as well-a quaint old come-all-ye lyric-
mss george essler

the bold norwestmen

come all ye bold norwestmen who plow the rag-
    ing main--

attend unto this tragedy while i relate the same
tis of the lady wellington at anchor where she lay

off queen charlottes island northwest of
    amerikay

it was in the year one thousand seven hundred and
    ninety-one

great numbers of wild savages on board of our
    ship did come

and for to buy their furs of them our captain did
    begin

but mark what followed after before long time
    had been

       

twas aft upon our quarter deck two arms-chests
    there did stand

the keys had been left in them by our gunners
    careless hand

those savages they seized them and of them made a
    prize

oh lord we had no other arms for to defend
    our lives

twas aft upon our quarter deck our captain he did
    stand

with twenty of those wild savages their naked
    knives in hand

twenty of those wild savages their naked knives
    they drew

if he should flinch or budge an inch ready to run
     him through

our captain then perceiving this he unto them did
    say-

if you give back my keys again great sums to
    you ill pay-	

men have sung

 but the savages not fearing him they all drew
    forth their knives

 saying the ship shes ours now and you
    shall lose your lives

 our captain then perceiving this-the ship no
    longer ours-

 he called unto his sailors bold likewise his
    officers

 go down into my cabin boys some firearms youll
    find there

 see that they are well loaded mind that they
    dont miss fire

then down into his cabin we quickly did repair-

 but to our sad misfortune no firearms we found
    there

 except a brace of pistols two guns and one broad-
    sword-

 then instantly we did agree blow her up it
    was the word

then to acquaint our captain we quickly did repair

 and instantly he did agree blow her up into
    the air

 our powder was got ready our gun-rooms opened
    wide

 our souls committed to the lord our bodies to
    the tide

 those savages perceiving this they in our cabins
    rushed-

 our captain cried out follow them and after
    them we pushed

 ninety of these wild savages we killed upon the
    floor

 as many more we wounded and the rest jumped
    overboard

early on the next morning just by the break of day

 our captain called all hands on deck our anchor
    for to weigh

 with our cannon and small arms we on their town
    did play

 till they brought back those keys again which
    they had stole away

 now come all you bold norwestmen who plow the
    raging main-

 come listen to my tragedy while i conclude the
    same

 beware of all wild savages of them youll find no
    end

 and they will strive to take your life when you
    have been their friend
